 Zynq XC7Z010-CLG400A bootrom project
;
;  Tom Trebisky  5-2-2024

; The bootrom is 128k (from 0 to 0x0001ffff)
; After 0x18770 it is filled with an odd
; pattern that we can ignore: ffc5dead
;
; The ROM includes this timestamp:
;    Dec  1 2012 01:16:55
; What were you doing on December 1 of 2012 ?


; The disassembly is 32770 lines before we trim anything off.
; It is 25070 lines (more or less) after ditching the trailing ffc5dead lines.
; However, everything from 0xff14 and on looks like
; a mass of data (maybe an FPGA bitstream?)
; This cuts us down to "only" about 16,000 lines of code
; and there are other data blocks embedded in that.

; Some anonymous tips:
;
; JTAG comes up disabled, and is enabled by the bootrom after it copies a stub to high memory.
; look at Binary Ninja (https://binary.ninja/free/) it's a great tool.
; main is 0x1688, code is basically constant til 0xb648,
;  then there is a chunk from 0xfc80 to 0xff10
;  (this is the stub copied to high memory before locking away the rom).
;  RAM gets mapped to 0x70000 during bootrom execution. 

; Serial IO and UART.
; We are on the lookout for "XLNX_ZYNQ"
;  (and we found it at 0xa228)
;
; Uart0 base is 0xE000_0000
; Uart1 base is 0xE000_1000
; The Uart registers are like so:

;-  struct zynq_uart {
;-  
;-          vu32    control;	// 00
;-          vu32    mode;	// 04
;-          vu32    ie;		// 08
;-          vu32    id;		// 0c
;-  
;-          vu32    im;		// 10
;-          vu32    istatus;	// 14
;-          vu32    baud;	// 18
;-          vu32    rto;	// 1c
;-  
;-          vu32    rfifo;	// 20
;-          vu32    mcontrol;	// 24
;-          vu32    mstatus;	// 28
;-          vu32    cstatus;	// 2c
;-  
;-          vu32    fifo;	// 30
;-          vu32    baud_div;	// 34
;-          vu32    fc_delay;	// 38
;-          int     __pad1[2];
;-          vu32    tfifo;	// 44

; ------------------------------------------------
; Some notes on ARM instructions:
; This is in general a refresher course for myself since I
; tend to work on this, then go away for long periods and
; then come back and want to get up to speed.
;
; The stack grows to lower addresses.
; The sp points to the "top" of the stack.
; push does *(sp-4) = x then sp = sp - 4
; pop does x = *sp then sp = sp + 4
; push and pop are aliases for:
;	str r3, [sp, #-4]!
;	ldr r3, [sp], #4
;
; Subroutine calls are done via the "bl" instruction.
; This puts the pc (return address) into the lr
; and then sets the pc to the target address.
; Note that this has nothing to do with the stack.
; A leaf routine can simply return using "bx lr"
; However if the called routine wants to call other
; routines (i.e. is not a leaf routine), it must first
; save the lr or else it will be overwritten.
; A cute trick is to push the lr
; (often along with other registers),
; then to return (and restore those registers) one can
; use a single instruction like:
;	pop	{r4, pc}
;
; note that a pop with "lr" at the end does not return
; it just restores the register.  Consider this:
;	pop	{r4, r5, r6, r7, r8, lr}
;	b	0xb000		; io_write
;
; The compiler sometimes generates code like this when the
; last thing in a routine is to call another.
; Rather than a "bl" to make that final call, it
; just branches there with the "lr" register holding
; the return address for the "parent" routine.
; a nice shortcut and imporant to know about in this code.

; mrc and mcr -- these are the coprocessor interface instructions
; that annoy me so much.
; I have translated the usual nonsense to mnemonic names.
; mrc is "send a coprocessor command and get data
;  like this, which reads the "multiprocessor affinity register"
;		mrc	fp, MPIDR
; mcr is "send a coprocessor command and also send data
;  like this, which write to the translation table base register
;		mcr     r0, TTBR0

; bic instruction - bit clear.  performs AND with ~MASK
; mvn instruction - loads ~data
;  These two are equivalent:
;    mov r3, #0xffffffff
;    mvn r3, #0
;  The first could not even be generated, but a good assembler
;   would transform it to the second.
; cmn instruction is exactly the same as "cmp" except
;  that the second operand gets negated before the compare.
;  The point of this is that storing negative values as
;   immediate values is not convenient for the ARM.
;   you will usually see code like this:
; There are some fine points with "cmn" that you can read about
;  further along in these notes.
;		cmn	r0, #1

; ARM "pre-increment" notation.
; Note the instruction ldrb    r0, [r1, #1]!
; ARM docs call this a "pre-increment"
; this case reads from r1+1, but the trick is
; that it also modifies r1 to be r1+1 when done.
; So consider   "push {reg}"
; this is equivalent to str  reg, [sp, #4]!
; the register gets stored to sp+4 and
; sp gets incremented to sp+4.

; --- ARM addressing modes, some are trivial
; reg to reg -- mov r0, r1
; absolute   -- ldr r0, mem
; immediate  -- mov r0, #13
; indirect   -- ldr r0, [r2]
; double     -- ldr r0, [r2, r3]
;  w/ offset -- ldr r0, [r2, #4]
;  PC rel    -- ldr r0, [pc, #4]
;  - preinc  -- ldr r0, [r2, #4]!
;  - postinc -- ldr r0, [r2], #4
; w/ scaling -- ldr r0, [r2, r3, lsl #2]

; These are all obvious except the last 3.
; There is nothing special about PC relative beyond understanding
;  that the PC value used is the address of the current instruction
;  plus 8!!  You can verify this for yourself looking at this code
;  where objdump has calculated the address for you.
;
; preinc loads from r2+4 in the example shown,
;  then sets r2 to r2+4
; postinc loads from r2, then sets r2 to r2+4
;
; in the form with scaling it is the last register that gets shifted.
;   So the example: ldr r0, [r2, r3, lsl #2]
;   does this:  r0 = *(r2+r3<<2); in C syntax
;   The shift value can even be in an array!
;   There is a lot of this in this code.
;   (I am surprised things get this fancy in a RISC processor)

; ARM flags -- these are the first 5 bits of the CPSR
; These are:  NZCVQ

; One surprise for me is the C bit on subtraction.
; It gets set (for example) if we subtract 1 from 4!
; The ARM documents say:
;  The carry (C) flag is set when an operation results in a carry,
;  or when a subtraction results in no borrow.

; ------------------------------------------------
; ------------------------------------------------
; Addresses --
;
; OCM (on chip memory).
; We have 256K of ram and this 128K boot ROM
; a bunch of tricks go on, not all of which are documented.
;
; After reset (when this code is running), we find the ROM mapped to
; address 0, so it sits in the first 128K of the address space.
; It seems that the 256K of ram is at 0x40000 to 0x7ffff
; Certainly the 0x7xxxx region is ram, as it is used for the
;  stack and a variety of variables by the bootrom.
;
; Some other trickery goes on with the high addresses
;  like 0xffff0000 ...  The TRM says that the SRAM can be
;  mapped high or low with 64K granularity.  So a single 64K "page"
;  could be put up there -- the bootrom copies some code up there
;  to bounce through when it maps itself out of existence (somehow).
; The TRM says this is handled by slcr.OCM_CFG
;  see page 1570 of the TRM for this.
;  the base for the slcr is 0xF800_0000
;  OCM_CFG is at offset 0x910
;
; --------------------------------------
;
; I had begun sprinkling Address notes around in the code in the areas where
; I tripped over them, but decided we would be better served to
; collect them all, in order, in a nice reference section up front.
; Namely right here ---
; See page 112 of the TRM

; f800_0000 to f800_0bff are SLCR registers
;  (system level control registers) TRM page 114 and 1571
; f800_1000 to f880_ffff are PS registers
; Table 4-3 (page 114) covers SLCR (System-level control registers)
; Table 4-7 (page 116) covers the PS registers, as follows
; f800_1000 = triple timer 0
; f800_2000 = triple timer 1
; f800_3000 = DMAC secure
; f800_4000 = DMAC non-secure
; f800_5000 = system watchdog
; f800_6000 = DDR controller
; f800_7000 = DevC (device configuration)
; f800_8000 = AXI HP 0
; f800_9000 = AXI HP 1
; f800_A000 = AXI HP 2
; f800_B000 = AXI HP 3
; f800_C000 = OCM
; f800_D000 = eFuse

; 0xf800_07xx are MIO pin control (in the SLCR)
;  an array of 54 registers, one per pin
;  (we have MIO 0 to 53)
;  example: 07a4 is pin 41  0x7d4 is pin 53

; DevC (device configuration) collects a lot of useful stuff.
; I remember searching high and low for this when I was writing code
; to load a bitstream into the PL.  My notes from that project say to
; look at page 1144 (B.16) in the TRM.
; Appendix B is of vital interest at any event as it is entitled
;   "Register Details"

; eFuse is still a mystery.
; there is a lot in the TRM section 32 (Device Secure Boot)
; it is possible to require that th FSBL is encrypted
; and/or to require RSA authentication.
; fuses can permanently disable JTAG DAP and TAP
; details of the fuses and registers in the f800_d000 area
; is not present in the TRM.

; ====================================================
; Some notes on addresses in SRAM --
; The 64K chunk at 0x70000 is used as private
;  storage by the bootrom.  It places the stack here
;  as well as a variety of pointers and variables.
;
; Here is what I know so far:
;  0x70000 -- some kind of flag
;  0x70004 -- ?
;  0x70490 -- ?
;  0x70498 -- &BUF (where incoming data accumulates)
;  0x7068c -- pointer to data copy callback
;  0x70e90 -- ?
;  0x70ec0 -- ?
;  0x7d4a0 -- stack (grows to lower addresses)
;
; ---------------------------------------------
; ---------------------------------------------
;
; Execution starts right here at address zero.
; (and jumps right away to 0x44)
; This jump is followed by 7 interrupt vectors
; The first 6 just fall into the last.
; They all give boot_error 0x2600

00000000:	ea00000f 	b	0x44
00000004:	e320f000 	nop	{0}
00000008:	e320f000 	nop	{0}
0000000c:	e320f000 	nop	{0}
00000010:	e320f000 	nop	{0}
00000014:	e320f000 	nop	{0}
00000018:	e320f000 	nop	{0}
0000001c:	e10f0000 	mrs	r0, CPSR
00000020:	e3e0101f 	mvn	r1, #31
00000024:	e0012000 	and	r2, r1, r0
00000028:	e382201f 	orr	r2, r2, #31
0000002c:	e129f002 	msr	CPSR_fc, r2
00000030:	e59fd008 	ldr	sp, [pc, #8]	@ 0x40
00000034:	e3a00c26 	mov	r0, #9728	@ 0x2600
00000038:	ea0002f1 	b	0xc04		; boot_error ()

; spin
0000003c:	eafffffe 	b	0x3c

00000040:	0007d4a0	; initial stack

; First code to run is here.
00000044:	eb000022 	bl	0xd4		; setup caches
00000048:	ee070f15	mcr	r0, ICIALLU
0000004c:	ee070fd5	mcr	r0, BPIALL
00000050:	ee080f17	mcr	r0, TLBIALL

; read processor affinity register (core number in low 2 bits)
00000054:	ee10bfb0	mrc	fp, MPIDR
00000058:	e20bb00f 	and	fp, fp, #15
0000005c:	e35b0000 	cmp	fp, #0
00000060:	0a000002 	beq	0x70

; On the Zynq chip, both cores start up when it comes out of reset.
; Without some care, they would both run the bootrom code side by side
; which would lead to all kinds of bad things.
; So, we trap the second core (core 1) right here.
; It will stay in this spin loop until the endgame code
; plays an interesting game to divert it into a wait loop
; in high memory (for which, see the end_game routines).

00000064:	f57ff04f 	dsb	sy
00000068:	e320f002 	wfe
0000006c:	eafffffc 	b	0x64

; core 0 continues right along

; set up performance monitor registers
00000070:	ee190f1c	mrc	r0, PMCR
00000074:	e3800005 	orr	r0, r0, #5
00000078:	ee090f1c	mcr	r0, PMCR
0000007c:	ee190f1c	mrc	r0, PMCR

00000084:	ee090f1c	mcr	r0, PMCR

0000008c:	ee090f3c	mcr	r0, PMCNTENSET
00000090:	e10f0000 	mrs	r0, CPSR
00000094:	e3e0101f 	mvn	r1, #31
00000098:	e0012000 	and	r2, r1, r0
0000009c:	e382201f 	orr	r2, r2, #31
000000a0:	e129f002 	msr	CPSR_fc, r2

; set the initial stack
000000a4:	e59fd0b8 	ldr	sp, [pc, #184]	@ 0x164
000000a8:	e3e00000 	mvn	r0, #0
000000ac:	ee030f10	mcr	r0, DACR

; set the initial MMU tables
000000b0:	e59f00b0 	ldr	r0, [pc, #176]	@ 0x168 (0x10000)
000000b4:	ee020f10	mcr	r0, TTBR0

000000bc:	e3a00001 	mov	r0, #1

000000c4:	f57ff04f 	dsb	sy
000000c8:	f57ff06f 	isb	sy
000000cc:	ea0029a8 	b	0xa774

; spin (why? how do we get here?)
; there are quite a few of these scattered around,
; as if to "catch" the processor if it ever goes crazy??

000000d0:	eafffffe 	b	0xd0

; subroutine (called once from above)
; looks to me like it is invalidating the entire data cache.
; note: the data cache in the Zynq is 32K
000000d4:	f57ff05f 	dmb	sy
000000d8:	ee300f30	mrc	r0, CLIDR
000000dc:	e2103407 	ands	r3, r0, #0x7000000	; 0x700_0000
000000e0:	e1a03ba3 	lsr	r3, r3, #23		; >> 23
000000e4:	0a00001a 	beq	0x154
; The above extracts (and tests) the LoC (level of coherency)
000000e8:	e3a0a000 	mov	sl, #0			; sl = 0

; Uart0 base is 0xE000_0000
; Uart1 base is 0xE000_1000
; The Uart registers are like so:

;-  struct zynq_uart {
;-  
;-          vu32    control;	// 00
;-          vu32    mode;	// 04
;-          vu32    ie;		// 08
;-          vu32    id;		// 0c
;-  
;-          vu32    im;		// 10
;-          vu32    istatus;	// 14
;-          vu32    baud;	// 18
;-          vu32    rto;	// 1c
;-  
;-          vu32    rfifo;	// 20
;-          vu32    mcontrol;	// 24
;-          vu32    mstatus;	// 28
;-          vu32    cstatus;	// 2c
;-  
;-          vu32    fifo;	// 30
;-          vu32    baud_div;	// 34
;-          vu32    fc_delay;	// 38
;-          int     __pad1[2];
;-          vu32    tfifo;	// 44

; Note - I never see this code using "tfifo" or "rfifo"
;   It does write to plain "fifo" to send the prompt

; subroutine - 2 calls
; Some kind of flag in 0x70000
; we bail out if it is non-zero
; we set the flag if we run
000006a0:	e3003000 	movw	r3, #0
000006a4:	e92d4010 	push	{r4, lr}
000006a8:	e3403007 	movt	r3, #7		; r3 = 0x70000
000006ac:	e5932000 	ldr	r2, [r3]
000006b0:	e3520000 	cmp	r2, #0		; check flag
000006b4:	18bd8010 	popne	{r4, pc}
; set up uart1
; 0xe0001018 is uart1 baud
; 0xe0001034 is uart1 baud div
; 0xe0001004 is uart1 mode
; 0xe0001004 is uart1 control

; the call to 0xb000 does this (with a dmb): str	r1, [r0]
; i.e. it stores what is in r1 at the addess given by r0

000006b8:	e3a0c001 	mov	ip, #1
000006bc:	e3010018	movw	r0, #0x1018
000006c0:	e583c000 	str	ip, [r3]	; set flag
000006c4:	e3a01011 	mov	r1, #0x11
000006c8:	e34e0000	movt	r0, #0xe000
000006cc:	eb002a4b 	bl	0xb000		; io_write ( 0xe000_1018, 0x11 )

000006d0:	e3010034	movw	r0, #0x1034
000006d4:	e3a01006 	mov	r1, #6
000006d8:	e34e0000	movt	r0, #0xe000
000006dc:	eb002a47 	bl	0xb000		; io_write ( 0xe000_1034, 6 )

000006e0:	e3010004	movw	r0, #0x1004

000006ec:	eb002a43 	bl	0xb000		; io_write ( 0xe000_1004, 0x20 )

; subroutine - many calls
;
; boot_error ( errno )
; ( note that we also have bootrom_error () which is more bare-bones )
;
; argument (in r0) is an error number like 0x2501 or 0x2001
; it seems that all error numbers have 0x2xxx,
; the second digit may be the subsystem
;  (such as 0x250x for the secret uart)
; the last two digits are the specific error within
;  the subsystem
;
; Note that the TRM has a fairly big (but not complete)
; list of these on pages 199 to 202

; 0xf800d010 is in eFuse

; uxtb - extract a byte and extend zeros
;   it does not change flags
; sub versus subs -- subs changes flags, sub does not
; not the addressing mode in: strb    r1, [r3], #1
; in C syntax this does:  *r3++ = r1
; the store goes to the current address in r3, then r3 gets incremented

; subroutine "memset"
;  a typical call has:
;	r0 = address
;	r1 = 0 (value)
;	r2 = 16 (count)

; subroutine - "copy" - 3 calls
; looks like a copy of a null terminated string
; r0 is destination, r1 is source
; we are free to trash r2 and r3 (and r1)
; r0 will return same as when called.
;
; Note the instruction ldrb    r0, [r1, #1]!
; ARM docs call this a "pre-increment"
; this case reads from r1+1, but the trick is
; that it also modifies r1 to be r1+1 when done.

; read from 0xf800025c
; This gives the boot mode settings.
; registers at 0xf800_0200 are "reset control and status"
; See page 1622 of the TRM.  We get 4 "boot mode" bits
; that are sampled and stored at power on reset.
; See page 165-166 of the TRM.
; Boot_mode[0] = MIO5
; Boot_mode[1] = MIO3
; Boot_mode[2] = MIO4
; Boot_mode[3] = MIO2
; Boot_mode[4] = MIO6 (PLL bypass)
;
; This register gives us bit 4 as PLL bypass
; then 4 bits of boot mode in bits 3:0
;
; NOTE !! !!
; This hairball reordering of the bits happens on chip between
; the MIO pins and this register.

00001788:	e3a00f97 	mov	r0, #604	@ 0x25c
0000178c:	e34f0800	movt	r0, #0xf800
00001790:	eb002607 	bl	0xafb4		; io_read
00001794:	e1a05000 	mov	r5, r0		; save boot-mode

; A curious thing to do at this moment
00001798:	ebfffbe2 	bl	0x728		; disable L2 cache

; write 0x20 to 0xe000d038
; 0xe000_d000 are the qspi registers
; this sets the loopback delay value.
; Seems like an odd place to be setting this.

0000179c:	e30d0038	movw	r0, #0xd038
000017a0:	e3a01020 	mov	r1, #32
000017a4:	e34e0000	movt	r0, #0xe000

000017a8:	e2055007 	and	r5, r5, #7	; mask to 3 bits
000017ac:	eb002613 	bl	0xb000		; io_write

; ----------------------------------------
; subroutine - MANY calls
; boron ( source, dest, N )
;
; this calls a callback that has been registered
; by whatever boot method was selected

; Set MIO pins for uart
; MIO pins are in the SLCR
; see page 52 in the TRM for a cute table
; page 1570 covers the SLCR, 
; f800_07c0 is pin 48
; f800_07c4 is pin 49
; See page 1679, 1680 for these specific pins
; as you might expect this sets pin 48 to TxD and 49 to RxD
; (also some values get set in RAM here)

0000a1f8:	e3a00d1f 	mov	r0, #0x7c0	; ..

; getc() returns the character in r0

; 0x18748 has the string "BAUD\0"
; this code is looking for the character 'B'

# Now get the first integer

; Finally, we have the third value in r5

; the above code did uart->ctrl |= 0x28

; The following routine is a "callback" used by the uart loader
; It is fundamentally a wrapper around memcpy that performs
; various checks.

; Here are some notes on it from "404" --

; The intent of this routine is straightforward:
;  Grab nbytes from offset bytes deep into the ROM image, and write them to dest.

; The RSBGT weirdness at 0xa5ac is a check for the span of the request
;  (combined with the check at 0xa5a8) , but is redundant and doesn't account
;  for some wraparounds anyway.
; The interesting part is the source address calculation:
;  There is a check, but it is just for the upper bound, which we already know
;  is constrained by earlier logic. Otherwise, it just adds our r0_offset argument
;  directly. That's nice - if we fully control r0_offset at any point where r1_dest
;  is something that persists after the ROM has locked itself down
;  (which is kinda the whole idea of a bootrom...),
;  we can stash stuff to look at later, as long as it lives below 0x70000.

; Aside: The same logic exists in both NOR and QSPI boot processes, with mildly fewer constrains.

; Note that OCM is mapped to 0x70000 while the bootrom is running.

; uart_copydata ( source, dest, N )

0000a578:	e92d4008 	push	{r3, lr}
0000a57c:	e3003488	movw	r3, #0x488
0000a580:	e3403007 	movt	r3, #7		; 0x70488
0000a584:	e3a0c001 	mov	ip, #1		; ip = 1
0000a588:	e5933000 	ldr	r3, [r3]	; r3 = *0x70488 = &buf
0000a58c:	e340c007 	movt	ip, #7		; 0x70001
0000a590:	e0833000 	add	r3, r3, r0	; r3 += r0 (arg1)
0000a594:	e153000c 	cmp	r3, ip
0000a598:	8a000007 	bhi	0xa5bc		; (return)

0000a59c:	e243c807 	sub	ip, r3, #0x70000	; ip = r3 - 0x70000
0000a5a0:	e24ce001 	sub	lr, ip, #1		; lr = ip - 1
0000a5a4:	e08e0002 	add	r0, lr, r2		; r0 = lr + N
0000a5a8:	e3500000 	cmp	r0, #0
0000a5ac:	c0602002 	rsbgt	r2, r0, r2		; N -+ r0 (maybe)

0000a5b0:	e1a00001 	mov	r0, r1		; dest = r1 (exactly as in arg)
0000a5b4:	e1a01003 	mov	r1, r3		; source = r3
0000a5b8:	ebffdb9c 	bl	0x1430		; memcpy ( dest, src, count )

0000a5bc:	e3a00000 	mov	r0, #0		; return 0
0000a5c0:	e8bd8008 	pop	{r3, pc}

; So, what is this SCTLR register?
; It is the ARM "system control register"
; It has bits to enable caches, control alignment handling
; and other basic aspects of the processor
;
; Below here we have a "library" of ARM housekeeping instructions,
; many of which are never called.

; subroutine
; this calls the above, then disables caches via the SCTLR
0000b22c:	e92d4008 	push	{r3, lr}
0000b230:	ebffffc3 	bl	0xb144
0000b234:	ee110f10	mrc	r0, SCTLR
0000b238:	e3c03004 	bic	r3, r0, #4
0000b23c:	ee013f10	mcr	r3, SCTLR
0000b240:	f57ff04f 	dsb	sy
0000b244:	e8bd8008 	pop	{r3, pc}

; ===============================================
; OCM --- short course
;  We have 256 bytes of SRAM and 128K of bootrom code.
;  Let's talk about 64K chunks
;  For the SRAM we have A, B, C, and D
;  From the bootrom we have R0 and R1 lets say.
;  These get mapped into the address space in various ways
;   at various times.
;  The interesting spots in the address space can be talked
;   about in the following way, again dealing with 64K chunks.
;  Lets think about the first 8 chunks of address space
;   and call them 0, 1, 2, ... 7
;  We need to also talk about the last 64k (0xffff_xxxx),
;   and let's just call it "F"
; On reset, we deduce the following - 
;  the bootrom is at zero so R0 is at A-0 and R1 is at A-1
;  the SRAM is 4*64K up, i.e. A is at A-4, B is at A-5, .. D is at A-7
;  This is special and undocumented, and changes when user code runs.
; The bootrom uses the last 64k chunk (D) at A-7 (address 0x7_0000)
;  for its own purposes.  The stack is here, as well as a variety of
;  variables.
; This means that only 3*64K is available for user code (the FSBL)
;  This size limit of 0x3_0000 is enforced by the bootrom.
; So while the bootrom is running, it places the user code (FSBL)
;  into SRAM at address base 0x4_0000.
; Finally when we get to what I call the "end game", several
;  things happen.  One is that the bootrom remaps chunk "D"
;  (which was previously at 0x7_0000) to address "F" (0xffff_xxxx)
; The bootrom stack and its collection of variables thusly move up
;  there, but the bootrom seems to never touch them once it does
;  the remapping of this address.
; Finally, the bootrom does something (and I have yet to figure out what)
;  that moves SRARM chunks A, B, and C to address base 0, hiding the
;  bootrom code itself
; To do this, the bootrom copies code into the "F" segment and jumps into
;  it, abandoning any need to run bootrom code based at address 0.

; ===============================================

; subroutine -- called from many places.
; end_game2()
; several calls have r0 = 0xffff_ff20
; one has r0 = 0x8000_0000

0000fc80:	e59f128c 	ldr	r1, [pc, #652]	@ 0xff14 ; (00070000)
0000fc84:	e59f228c 	ldr	r2, [pc, #652]	@ 0xff18 ; (0007d4a0)
0000fc88:	e3a03000 	mov	r3, #0
0000fc8c:	ebffeabe 	bl	0xa78c		; fill_mem ()
0000fc90:	e3a0207f 	mov	r2, #127	@ 0x7f
0000fc94:	ea00000c 	b	0xfccc		; below in "end_game1"

; ===============================================
; subroutine -- "lockdown()"
; One error case comes here to die and spin (one call)
; first, clear the image in memory (security?)
0000fc98:	e3a01701 	mov	r1, #262144	@ 0x40000
0000fc9c:	e59f2274 	ldr	r2, [pc, #628]	@ 0xff18 ; (0007d4a0)
0000fca0:	e3a03000 	mov	r3, #0
0000fca4:	ebffeab8 	bl	0xa78c		; fill_mem ()

; 0xf8007000 is DevConfig
; This first register is the control register.
; See TRM page 1146.
; setting bit 31 like this "forces the PS into secure lockdown"

; ===============================================
; ===============================================
;
; ENDGAME
;
; subroutine -- One call from 0x1134 (at 0xfcb8) -- end_game1()
; (BUT note the path from end_game2() directly above).
; This is the end game transferring to the loaded image.
; It looks like r0 has the start address of the loaded image

; Clear memory in page "D" of SRAM (at 0x70000
0000fcb8:	e59f1254 	ldr	r1, [pc, #596]	@ 0xff14 ; (00070000)
0000fcbc:	e59f2254 	ldr	r2, [pc, #596]	@ 0xff18 ; (0007d4a0)
0000fcc0:	e3a03000 	mov	r3, #0
0000fcc4:	ebffeab0 	bl	0xa78c		; fill_mem ()
0000fcc8:	e3a02000 	mov	r2, #0		; no JTAG (see below)

; Common code follows for end_game1() and end_game2()
; the difference is the value in r2 (either 0 or 0x7f)
; end_game1() with the zero value will disallow JTAG in the
; image it boots, calls via eng_game2() will allow it.

; save the start address in "lr"
0000fccc:	e1a0e000 	mov	lr, r0		; lr = r0 = start_address

; Read the Devconfig CTRL register
; We either OR with 0x0 or 0x7f
; The latter case enables debug, including the ARM DAP (for JTAG)
; note that r2 and r3 don't get messed with so this can be done later
0000fcd0:	e59f3244 	ldr	r3, [pc, #580]	@ 0xff1c ; (f8007000)
0000fcd4:	e5935000 	ldr	r5, [r3]
0000fcd8:	e1822005 	orr	r2, r2, r5
; r2 and r3 are "loaded up" for later.

; caches off
0000fcdc:	e3a04000 	mov	r4, #0
0000fce0:	ee014f10	mcr	r4, SCTLR
0000fce4:	f57ff04f 	dsb	sy
0000fce8:	f57ff06f 	isb	sy

; invalidate I cache and branch predictors

0000fcf0:	ee070fd5	mcr	r0, BPIALL
0000fcf4:	f57ff04f 	dsb	sy

; invalidate TLB

0000fd00:	f57ff04f 	dsb	sy

; fetch/modify/save for the "multiboot address"
0000fd08:	e59f5210 	ldr	r5, [pc, #528]	@ 0xff20 ; (f800702c)
0000fd0c:	e3a01903 	mov	r1, #0xc000

; Fetch the base address of Devconfig (0xf8007000)
; add a 0x28 offset, so r0 = 0xf8007028
; This address will be used later (in the stub)
; This address is undocumented in the TRM (it jumps from 0x24 to 0x2c)

0000fd1c:	e59f01f8 	ldr	r0, [pc, #504]	@ 0xff1c ; (f8007000)
0000fd20:	e3a01028 	mov	r1, #0x28
0000fd24:	e0800001 	add	r0, r0, r1

; This is loaded up for much later
0000fd28:	e3e01000 	mvn	r1, #0		; 0xffff_ffff
; now we have r0, r1, r2, and r3 "loaded up"

; Write 0x8 to the OCM config register.
; this maps the fourth (and last) 64K segment of the 256K
; OCM sram to high addresses (0xffff0000 ...)
; If I am guessing right about what is going on while the bootrom
; is running, this is the segment that has up to now been at 0x70000.
; --------
; This changes the world, after this there should be no more access
; to things in 0x7xxxx, in particular the stack.
; Note that as far as I can see it would be perfectly OK to
; now access the stuff that used to be at 0x7_0000 via 0xffff_0000 addresses,
; but the bootrom never seems to do that.
; This may simply be "convenience" as it would be difficult to configure a
; compiler to deal with trickery like thos.
; Anything we need at 0xffff_xxxx addresses we now write fresh.

0000fd2c:	e59f51f0 	ldr	r5, [pc, #496]	@ 0xff24 ; (0xf800910 - OCM_CFG)
0000fd30:	e3a06008 	mov	r6, #8
0000fd34:	e5856000 	str	r6, [r5]

; We will write this value later to the OCM_CFG register
; Note that the 0x10 bit is essentially undocumented in the TRM
; on page 1690, they label this bit as "SWAP" and simply state
; that you should always write what you read (i.e. maintain it).
; ?? Could this be the magic bit that unmaps the bootrom ??
0000fd38:	e3a06018 	mov	r6, #0x18	; r6 = 0x18

; set up the "sentinel" value for the watch loop at 0xffff_ff2c

0000fd3c:	e3e0800f 	mvn	r8, #15		; r8 = 0xffff_fff0
0000fd40:	e3e090d3 	mvn	r9, #0xd3	; r9 = 0xffff_ff2c
0000fd44:	e5889000 	str	r9, [r8]

; set up copy 1
0000fd48:	e59fa1d8 	ldr	sl, [pc, #472]	@ 0xff28 ; (0000fed4)
0000fd4c:	e3e0b0df 	mvn	fp, #0xdf	; fp = 0xffff_ff20
0000fd50:	e59fc1d4 	ldr	ip, [pc, #468]	@ 0xff2c ; (0000ff10)
0000fd54:	e04cc00a 	sub	ip, ip, sl

; COPY - 1
; A copy loop (just like below) copy from sl to fp -- ip is the count (0x3c)
; i.e. it copies to 0xffff_ff20
0000fd58:	e24cc004 	sub	ip, ip, #4
0000fd5c:	e79ad00c 	ldr	sp, [sl, ip]
0000fd60:	e78bd00c 	str	sp, [fp, ip]
0000fd64:	e35c0000 	cmp	ip, #0
0000fd68:	0a000000 	beq	0xfd70		; break
0000fd6c:	eafffff9 	b	0xfd58		; loop

; Set up copy 2
; note: sl is r10, fp is r11, ip is r12
0000fd70:	e59fa1b8 	ldr	sl, [pc, #440]	@ 0xff30 ; (0000fe1c)
0000fd74:	e59fb1b8 	ldr	fp, [pc, #440]	@ 0xff34 ; (fffffe00)
0000fd78:	e59fc1b8 	ldr	ip, [pc, #440]	@ 0xff38 ; (0000fed0)
0000fd7c:	e04cc00a 	sub	ip, ip, sl

; COPY - 2
; A copy loop.  Copies from sl to fp (i.e. from 0xfe1c to 0xfffffe00)
; cute that it uses the sp as a temporary register
; (that is a hint that the code is all done with the stack at this point).
; this copies to 0xffff_fe00

0000fd80:	e24cc004 	sub	ip, ip, #4
0000fd84:	e79ad00c 	ldr	sp, [sl, ip]
0000fd88:	e78bd00c 	str	sp, [fp, ip]
0000fd8c:	e35c0000 	cmp	ip, #0
0000fd90:	0a000000 	beq	0xfd98		; break
0000fd94:	eafffff9 	b	0xfd80		; loop

; 0xf8000244 is CPU reset and control
0000fd98:	e59fa19c 	ldr	sl, [pc, #412]	@ 0xff3c ; (f8000244)
; now sl = 0xf800_0244 for later

0000fd9c:	e3a04000 	mov	r4, #0		; r4 = 0;
; now r4 = 0 for later (base address of user image)

0000fda0:	e59f9198 	ldr	r9, [pc, #408]	@ 0xff40 ; (e3e0f0d3)
; now r9 is an instruction we patch in at that address (see below)

; *(0xffff_ff00) = 0xf800_0004
; 0xf8000004 is the lock register
0000fda4:	e3e070ff 	mvn	r7, #0xff	; 0xffff_ff00
0000fda8:	e59f8194 	ldr	r8, [pc, #404]	@ 0xff44 ; (f8000004)
0000fdac:	e5878000 	str	r8, [r7]

; *(0xffff_ff04) = 0000767b
0000fdb0:	e3e070fb 	mvn	r7, #0xfb	; 0xffff_ff04
0000fdb4:	e59f818c 	ldr	r8, [pc, #396]	@ 0xff48 ; (0000767b)
0000fdb8:	e5878000 	str	r8, [r7]

; 0xf800025c is BOOT mode
; read it and mask out lower 4 bits
; save to ffff_ff18

0000fdbc:	e59f7188 	ldr	r7, [pc, #392]	@ 0xff4c ; (f800025c)
0000fdc0:	e5978000 	ldr	r8, [r7]
0000fdc4:	e208800f 	and	r8, r8, #15	; 0xf
0000fdc8:	e3e070e7 	mvn	r7, #0xe7	; 0xffff_ff18
0000fdcc:	e5878000 	str	r8, [r7]

; The value 8 is JTAG boot and PLL bypassed
0000fdd0:	e3580008 	cmp	r8, #8
0000fdd4:	0a000001 	beq	0xfde0

0000fdd8:	e35e0102 	cmp	lr, #0x80000000
0000fddc:	1a00000b 	bne	0xfe10

; *(0xffff_ff08) = 0xf8000900
; 0xf8000900 is level shifter enable
0000fde0:	e3e070f7 	mvn	r7, #0xf7	; 0xffff_ff08
0000fde4:	e59f8164 	ldr	r8, [pc, #356]	@ 0xff50 ; (f8000900)
0000fde8:	e5878000 	str	r8, [r7]

; *(0xffff_ff0c) = 15
0000fdec:	e3e070f3 	mvn	r7, #0xf3	; 0xffff_ff0c
0000fdf0:	e3a0800f 	mov	r8, #15
0000fdf4:	e5878000 	str	r8, [r7]

; *(0xffff_ff10) = 0xf8000240
; 0xf8000240 is FPGA reset and control
0000fdf8:	e3e070ef 	mvn	r7, #0xef	; 0xffff_ff10
0000fdfc:	e59f8150 	ldr	r8, [pc, #336]	@ 0xff54 ; (f8000240)
0000fe00:	e5878000 	str	r8, [r7]

; *(0xffff_ff14) = 0
0000fe04:	e3e070eb 	mvn	r7, #0xeb	; 0xffff_ff14
0000fe08:	e3a08000 	mov	r8, #0
0000fe0c:	e5878000 	str	r8, [r7]

; shortcut branch to here from above
0000fe10:	f57ff04f 	dsb	sy
0000fe14:	f57ff06f 	isb	sy
0000fe18:	e12fff1b 	bx	fp		; jump to 0xfffffe00

; The above (the bx fp) jumps into the block of code we copied
; to 0xfffffe00 -- which follows directly below.
;
; and Below here is what we copy!
; So this will be at 0xffff_fe00 when it runs via the jump immediately above
; -------------
; Once again, we have changed the world.
; We have abandoned the code in the bootrom and are running
; entirely in the "stub" code we copied into the final page (0xffff_0000)
; All the state we care about should be in registers
;
; This block of code gets copied into high RAM as shown
; by copy block COPY - 2 above.
; ---

; ** begin the first block, which is the usual endgame path

; r0 = 0xf800_0028 -- undocumented register in Devconfig
; r5 = 0xf800_0910 -- the OCM_CFG register

ffff_fe00: 0000fe1c:	e5801000 	str	r1, [r0]	; *(0xf800_7028) = 0xffff_ffff
ffff_fe04: 0000fe20:	e5856000 	str	r6, [r5]	; *(0xf800_0910) = 0x18 
ffff_fe08: 0000fe24:	f57ff04f 	dsb	sy
ffff_fe0c: 0000fe28:	f57ff06f 	isb	sy

; r4 is 0
; r9 is e3e0f0d3 -- this is an instruction we deposit
;  in location 0.  Near as I can tell "mvn pc, 0xffff_ff2c"
; This causes CPU 1 to enter the wait loop in the stub

ffff_fe10: 0000fe2c:	e594b000 	ldr	fp, [r4]	; save
ffff_fe14: 0000fe30:	e5849000 	str	r9, [r4]

; sl is f8000244
; this is in SLCR -- "cpu reset and control"
; this pulses the reset line for the other core (CPU 1)
; asserting then releasing it
ffff_fe18: 0000fe34:	e3a0c002 	mov	ip, #2
ffff_fe1c: 0000fe38:	e58ac000 	str	ip, [sl]	; *sl = 2
ffff_fe20: 0000fe3c:	e3a0c000 	mov	ip, #0
ffff_fe24: 0000fe40:	e58ac000 	str	ip, [sl]	; *sl = 0
ffff_fe28: 0000fe44:	f57ff04f 	dsb	sy
ffff_fe2c: 0000fe48:	f57ff06f 	isb	sy

; restore whatever was at address 0 (i.e. the user image)
;  now that we have CPU 1 running where we want it.
ffff_fe30: 0000fe4c:	e584b000 	str	fp, [r4]
ffff_fe34: 0000fe50:	f57ff04f 	dsb	sy
ffff_fe38: 0000fe54:	f57ff06f 	isb	sy

; ffff_ff18 holds boot mode (4 bits)
; the value 8 would be JTAG with PLL bypassed

ffff_fe3c: 0000fe58:	e3e040e7 	mvn	r4, #0xe7	; 0xffff_ff18
ffff_fe40: 0000fe5c:	e5947000 	ldr	r7, [r4]
ffff_fe44: 0000fe60:	e3570008 	cmp	r7, #8		; test *(0xffff_ff18)
ffff_fe48: 0000fe64:	0a000001 	beq	0xfe70

; This is strange.  How can we boldly branch to 0x8000_0000 as if it is
; user code?  The TRM says this address is a "general purpose access port to the PL",
; i.e. the FPGA fabric.  We may learn more by examining the path that brings this
; address our way.
ffff_fe4c: 0000fe68:	e35e0102 	cmp	lr, #0x80000000
ffff_fe50: 0000fe6c:	1a00000d 	bne	0xfea8

; *(0xffff_ff08) = *(0xffff_ff0c)
ffff_fe54: 0000fe70:	e3e040f7 	mvn	r4, #0xf7	; 0xffff_ff08
ffff_fe58: 0000fe74:	e5947000 	ldr	r7, [r4]
ffff_fe5c: 0000fe78:	e3e050f3 	mvn	r5, #0xf3	; 0xffff_ff0c
ffff_fe60: 0000fe7c:	e5958000 	ldr	r8, [r5]
ffff_fe64: 0000fe80:	e5878000 	str	r8, [r7]

; Test bootmode and take shortcut if it is "8"
;  if ( *(0xffff_ff18) == 8 ) jump
;  bootmode "8" is JTAG with PLL bypassed
ffff_fe68: 0000fe84:	e3e040e7 	mvn	r4, #0xe7	; 0xffff_ff18
ffff_fe6c: 0000fe88:	e5947000 	ldr	r7, [r4]
ffff_fe70: 0000fe8c:	e3570008 	cmp	r7, #8
ffff_fe74: 0000fe90:	0a000004 	beq	0xfea8

; Clear FPGA reset and control register
; 0xffff_ff10 = 0xf8000240
; 0xffff_ff14 = 0
;
; *(*0xffff_ff10) = *(0xffff_ff14) 
ffff_fe78: 0000fe94:	e3e040ef 	mvn	r4, #0xef	; 0xffff_ff10
ffff_fe7c: 0000fe98:	e5947000 	ldr	r7, [r4]
ffff_fe80: 0000fe9c:	e3e050eb 	mvn	r5, #0xeb	; 0xffff_ff14
ffff_fe84: 0000fea0:	e5958000 	ldr	r8, [r5]
ffff_fe88: 0000fea4:	e5878000 	str	r8, [r7]

; Write to the "lock register"
; This is the magic value that should be written.
; Doing this locks all the SLCR registers
;  (locking means they are write protected)
; They can be unlocked by writing 0xdf0d to the
;   unlock register at 0xf800_0008
; See TRM pages 1576 - 1577
;
; 0xffff_ff00 = 0xf800_0004 (the lock register)
; 0xffff_ff04 = 0x0000_767b
;
; *(*0xffff_ff00) = *(0xffff_ff04)
ffff_fe8c: 0000fea8:	e3e040ff 	mvn	r4, #0xff	; 0xffff_ff00
ffff_fe90: 0000feac:	e5947000 	ldr	r7, [r4]
ffff_fe94: 0000feb0:	e3e050fb 	mvn	r5, #0xfb	; 0xffff_ff04
ffff_fe98: 0000feb4:	e5958000 	ldr	r8, [r5]
ffff_fe9c: 0000feb8:	e5878000 	str	r8, [r7]

ffff_fea0: 0000febc:	f57ff04f 	dsb	sy
ffff_fea4: 0000fec0:	f57ff06f 	isb	sy

ffff_fea8: 0000fec4:	e5832000 	str	r2, [r3]	; enable JTAG (maybe)
ffff_feac: 0000fec8:	f57ff04f 	dsb	sy
ffff_feb0: 0000fecc:	e12fff1e 	bx	lr
; The above jumps to user code (FSBL)
; and should never return

; spin
0000fed0:	eafffffe 	b	0xfed0

; ====================================================
; Here is the second block (handled by COPY - 1
; It has two loops.
; The first at 0xffff_ff20 is a place for the processor
;  (Core 0) to come and die, no doubt on severe
;  errors of some kind.
; The second (at 0xffff_ff2c) is where the second core
;  (Core 1) will be parked when control is transfered
;  to the user code (FSBL)

; This block of code gets copied into high RAM as shown
; by copy block COPY - 1 above.
; ---

; spin
ffff_ff20: 0000fed4:	f57ff04f 	dsb	sy
ffff_ff24: 0000fed8:	e320f002 	wfe
ffff_ff28: 0000fedc:	eafffffc 	b	0xfed4

; ======================================================
; Stub code to hold processor waiting to be signaled
; by someone writing to 0xffff_fff0
; Anything other than  0xffff_ff2c will "wake" this up.
; and cause it to go there.
;
; You might think that the second core (core 1) might get parked
; here, but I don't yet see how that happens.

; The sentinel is set up at 0xfd40 above as follows:
; 0000fd40:	e3e090d3 	mvn	r9, #0xd3	; r9 = 0xffff_ff2c
; Note that the mvn instruction creates the "not" of the immediate value,
; so 0xd3  (1100 0011) becomes 0x2c (0011 1100)
; but the cmn instruction deals with "negative", not "not".
; so 0xd4 becomes 0x2c -- how about that?
;
; There are more tricky issues with CMN -- assume nothing and
;  read the ARM documents with care.
;
; https://devblogs.microsoft.com/oldnewthing/20210607-00/?p=105288

ffff_ff2c: 0000fee0:	f57ff04f 	dsb	sy
ffff_ff30: 0000fee4:	e320f002 	wfe
ffff_ff34: 0000fee8:	e3e0000f 	mvn	r0, #15		; 0xffff_fff0
ffff_ff38: 0000feec:	e590e000 	ldr	lr, [r0]
ffff_ff3c: 0000fef0:	e37e00d4 	cmn	lr, #212	@ 0xd4
ffff_ff40: 0000fef4:	0afffff9 	beq	0xfee0

ffff_ff44: 0000fef8:	ee070f15	mcr	r0, ICIALLU
ffff_ff48: 0000fefc:	ee070fd5	mcr	r0, BPIALL
ffff_ff4c: 0000ff00:	ee080f17	mcr	r0, TLBIALL
ffff_ff50: 0000ff04:	e3a04000 	mov	r4, #0
ffff_ff54: 0000ff08:	ee014f10	mcr	r4, SCTLR
ffff_ff58: 0000ff0c:	e12fff1e 	bx	lr

; spin
0000ff10:	eafffffe 	b	0xff10

; END of code

; It all looks like data from here on.
0000ff14:	00070000
0000ff18:	0007d4a0
0000ff1c:	f8007000
0000ff20:	f800702c
0000ff24:	f8000910	; slcr.OCM_CFG
0000ff28:	0000fed4
0000ff2c:	0000ff10
0000ff30:	0000fe1c
0000ff34:	fffffe00
0000ff38:	0000fed0
0000ff3c:	f8000244
0000ff40:	e3e0f0d3
0000ff44:	f8000004
0000ff48:	0000767b
0000ff4c:	f800025c
0000ff50:	f8000900
0000ff54:	f8000240

; filler
0000ff58:	ffffffff
0000ff5c:	ffffffff
...
...
0000fff8:	ffffffff
0000fffc:	ffffffff

; These are MMU translation tables (MMU1)
